Foreword

It's a huge responsibility to present a scientific work of this magnitude. However, I agreed to do it with even more pride that the author of the book is very close to me. This probably offered me the opportunity to have witnessed the commitment to do the job, but also to follow personally its evolution, to attend all stages of its creation, not to mention the hardships the author was subject to, as well as ten years of hard work. Who would have guessed this? This work adds to the African lexicographic heritage; it is devoted to the Sango language, spoken by a minority people locked in the heart of Africa; this lingua franca became a national and then an official language and is perceived by its users as an instrument of national development. 
This work does not lack originality which lies first in the density of its inputs. It took years to investigate, collect words, inquire about their meaning, discuss them with users of all ages, and find synonyms, to finally reject or adopt them after the last required notice of her husband. But it's not only that. A regular consultation of the scientific literature, botanical, zoological, entomological, as well as that of specialists of all kinds, geologists, foresters, zoologists, botanists and, herbalists have completed the collection of insects, fruits, flowers and leaves that are carefully identified, hence also the use of scientific jargon. Originality is finally on the picture: the colour image of the environmental heritage of sangophones. This aspect is the innovation made to the Central African lexicography. It is a long-term task, a result of perceptible hard work: fish vendors and the author's colleagues know of her interest in taking pictures of Central African cultural realia during those long toiling years in which exudes the passion to make a work that could meet her own aspirations. Yet, it is only a lexicon we would say, but it's a lexicon of a new genre that is rich, colourful, and bilingual. This is a most valuable contribution to the development of the Sango language, a safe tool for the promotion and dissemination of this variety. More importantly, it is an instrument for the development of Sango. Beyond the linguistic fact, the bilingual Sango-English lexicon is a full expression of CAR culture, in all its richness and variety. What does not belong to that culture cannot be named. 
This work deserves admiration and delights. The author has done useful work and it will only benefit those who will have the opportunity to have it for various reasons. Our hope is that the author finds satisfaction that deserves such an effort. It's a resounding success.
Michel M. N. KOYT, Linguist

Introduction

This lexicon of usual words comprises images which allow an easy identification of the entities in question. Moreover, that facilitates the passage from one language to another. This document is not addressed only to the specialists. To make its use easy, the official orthography is used and the words are classified alphabetically. The entries are given according to the form of the singular and infinitive. The lexical category is indicated for each equivalent. 
1. 	Free variations 
The sango comprises many free variations at the vocalic and/or consonant level: 
Examples: 	 galâ / garâ; 
		 lörö / lölö / rörö 
		 mongênge: mangênge 
		 môlengê / mâlengê / mêlengê 
	 	 nzaï / nzabï 
		 mokonzi / makunzi 
		 balapäa / balakpäa 
	It is the same at the tonal level.
Example: 	 gïgî / gïgï / gîgî 
The variation can occur at the same time on the level of the vowels / consonants and the tone: 
Example: 	 makunzi / mokonzi / mokönzi 
In all cases, the form that is least marked on a regional level or that is most widespread is retained in order not to weigh down this lexicon unnecessarily. However, the variation can become so significant that the words seem to be different. In this case, the two forms are retained: 
Examples: 	 ôtoro / wôtoro 
		 hü / wu / wo 
		 mä / më 
		 tënë tî / tëtï 
2. 	 Compound words 
	The compound words are classified according to the alphabetical order of the first component. 
3. 	 Homonymy and Polysemy 
	When a word has several meanings (polysemic word), they are aligned one after the other.
Example: asa (Palî): to search, to scrape (Verb) 
	When the words have the same spelling but different meanings (homonyms), the various meanings are placed after the entry with numbers.
Example : längö : 1 (Palî): to sleep (Verb) 
	2 (Pandôo): 1 date, day (Noun) 
	2 sleep (Noun) 
	Finally, when the words have the same spelling but different pronounciations, they are entered one after the other.
Example: ngoro [ŋoro 1 (Palî) : to encircle, to go around, to surround (Verb)
2 (Pandôo) : perimeter (Noun)
ngoro [ŋɔrɔ (Pandôo) : silurid (Heterobranchus longifilis, Clariidae) (Noun)
